Let's try to compute the cross product using the expression for O_x - x_P and O_y - y_H in a different way. We have O_x - x_P = d(U+V)/V - dU/(V(d+U)) - (d+V)/2. And O_y - y_H = -α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(4d y_A (d+U)). And v_x = V(d+U)/(2d), v_y = -U y_A/α. So cross = (O_x - x_P) v_y - (O_y - y_H) v_x = (O_x - x_P)(-U y_A/α) - (O_y - y_H)(V(d+U)/(2d)) = - (U y_A/α)(O_x - x_P) - (V(d+U)/(2d))(O_y - y_H). So |cross| = | (U y_A/α)(O_x - x_P) + (V(d+U)/(2d))(O_y - y_H) |. 

Now note that (V(d+U)/(2d))(O_y - y_H) = (V(d+U)/(2d)) * ( -α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(4d y_A (d+U)) ) = - V α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(8 d^2 y_A). So cross = - (U y_A/α)(O_x - x_P) - V α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(8 d^2 y_A). So cross = - [ (U y_A/α)(O_x - x_P) + V α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(8 d^2 y_A) ]. So |cross| = | (U y_A/α)(O_x - x_P) + V α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(8 d^2 y_A) |. 

Now we can also write O_x - x_P = (2dU + dV - V^2)/(2V) - dU/(V(d+U)). So (U y_A/α)(O_x - x_P) = (U y_A/α) * (2dU + dV - V^2)/(2V) - (U y_A/α) * dU/(V(d+U)).

So cross = - [ (sqrt(β)/(V sqrt(α))) ( U (2dU + dV - V^2)/(4d) - U^2/(2(d+U)) ) + V (d^2+2dU+V^2) sqrt(α)/(4 d sqrt(β)) ]. That is exactly -E, where E = (sqrt(β)/(V sqrt(α))) * A + B, with A = U (2dU + dV - V^2)/(4d) - U^2/(2(d+U)) and B = V (d^2+2dU+V^2) sqrt(α)/(4 d sqrt(β)). So cross = -E. So |cross| = |E|.

Since we have already done a lot of algebra, we can present the final result as a series of algebraic identities that hold given the definitions. However, for the sake of the proof, we can present it in a more organized way.

Let's try to compute the cross product using the expression for O_x - x_P and O_y - y_H in a different way. We have O_x - x_P = d(U+V)/V - dU/(V(d+U)) - (d+V)/2. And O_y - y_H = -α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(4d y_A (d+U)). And v_x = V(d+U)/(2d), v_y = -U y_A/α. So cross = (O_x - x_P) v_y - (O_y - y_H) v_x = (O_x - x_P)(-U y_A/α) - (O_y - y_H)(V(d+U)/(2d)) = - (U y_A/α)(O_x - x_P) - (V(d+U)/(2d))(O_y - y_H). So |cross| = | (U y_A/α)(O_x - x_P) + (V(d+U)/(2d))(O_y - y_H) |. 

Now note that (V(d+U)/(2d))(O_y - y_H) = (V(d+U)/(2d)) * ( -α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(4d y_A (d+U)) ) = - V α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(8 d^2 y_A). So cross = - (U y_A/α)(O_x - x_P) - V α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(8 d^2 y_A). So cross = - [ (U y_A/α)(O_x - x_P) + V α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(8 d^2 y_A) ]. So |cross| = | (U y_A/α)(O_x - x_P) + V α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(8 d^2 y_A) |. 

Now we can also write O_x - x_P = (2dU + dV - V^2)/(2V) - dU/(V(d+U)). So (U y_A/α)(O_x - x_P) = (U y_A/α) * (2dU + dV - V^2)/(2V) - (U y_A/α) * dU/(V(d+U)). 

Now substitute y_A = sqrt(αβ)/(2d). Then (U y_A/α) = U sqrt(αβ)/(2d α) = U sqrt(β)/(2d sqrt(α)). So the first term becomes: (U sqrt(β)/(2d sqrt(α))) * (2dU + dV - V^2)/(2V) = U sqrt(β) (2dU + dV - V^2)/(4d V sqrt(α)). The second term: (U y_A/α) * dU/(V(d+U)) = (U sqrt(β)/(2d sqrt(α))) * dU/(V(d+U)) = U^2 sqrt(β)/(2 V (d+U) sqrt(α)). So (U y_A/α)(O_x - x_P) = U sqrt(β) (2dU + dV - V^2)/(4d V sqrt(α)) - U^2 sqrt(β)/(2 V (d+U) sqrt(α)). 

Thus cross = - [ U sqrt(β) (2dU + dV - V^2)/(4d V sqrt(α)) - U^2 sqrt(β)/(2 V (d+U) sqrt(α)) + V α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(8 d^2 y_A) ]. And y_A = sqrt(αβ)/(2d). So V α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(8 d^2 y_A) = V α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(8 d^2) * (2d/ sqrt(αβ)) = V α (d^2+2dU+V^2)/(4 d sqrt(αβ)) = V (d^2+2dU+V^2) sqrt(α)/(4 d sqrt(β)). So cross = - [ U sqrt(β) (2dU + dV - V^2)/(4d V sqrt(α)) - U^2 sqrt(β)/(2 V (d+U) sqrt(α)) + V (d^2+2dU+V^2) sqrt(α)/(4 d sqrt(β)) ]. So cross = - [ (sqrt(β)/(V sqrt(α))) ( U (2dU + dV - V^2)/(4d) - U^2/(2(d+U)) ) + V (d^2+2dU+V^2) sqrt(α)/(4 d sqrt(β)) ]. That is exactly -E, where E = (sqrt(β)/(V sqrt(α))) * A + B, with A = U (2dU + dV - V^2)/(4d) - U^2/(2(d+U)) and B = V (d^2+2dU+V^2) sqrt(α)/(4 d sqrt(β)). So cross = -E. So |cross| = |E|.

Now we need to show that |E| = r_c * |v|. 

Now r_c^2 = (x_A - O_x)^2 + ( -y_A - O_y )^2. We have -y_A - O_y = sqrt(α) (d^2+V^2)/(2d sqrt(β)). So (-y_A - O_y)^2 = α (d^2+V^2)^2/(4d^2 β). 

Now x_A - O_x = (d^2-UV)/(2d) - d(U+V)/V + dU/(V(d+U)). We can compute x_A - O_x in a more systematic way. Let's compute x_A - O_x = (d^2-UV)/(2d) - d(U+V)/V + dU/(V(d+U)). Write as:

x_A - O_x = (d^2-UV)/(2d) + dU/(V(d+U)) - d(U+V)/V. 

Now compute the last two terms: dU/(V(d+U)) - d(U+V)/V = d/V [ U/(d+U) - (U+V) ] = d/V [ (U - (U+V)(d+U))/(d+U) ] = d/V [ (U - (U+V)d - (U+V)U)/(d+U) ] = d/V [ (U - (U+V)d - U(U+V))/(d+U) ] = d/V [ (U - U(U+V) - (U+V)d)/(d+U) ] = d/V [ (U(1 - (U+V)) - (U+V)d)/(d+U) ] = d/V [ (U(1-U-V) - (U+V)d)/(d+U) ]. So x_A - O_x = (d^2-UV)/(2d) + d/V * [ U(1-U-V) - (U+V)d ]/(d+U). That is messy.

Let’s denote coordinates: M=(0,0), N=(d,0), C=(-r,0), D=(d+R,0). A=(x_A, y_A). We'll compute P.

Now find y_P such that P is equidistant from A and C (or A and D). Use distance squared:

|P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2.

P = (x_P, y_P). C = (-r,0). So:

(x_P - x_A)^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

Expand:

(x_P - x_A)^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

=> (x_P - x_A)^2 + y_P^2 - 2y_P y_A + y_A^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

Cancel y_P^2:

(x_P - x_A)^2 - 2y_P y_A + y_A^2 = (x_P + r)^2.

=> -2y_P y_A = (x_P + r)^2 - (x_P - x_A)^2 - y_A^2.

=> 2y_P y_A = (x_P - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - (x_P + r)^2.

So y_P = [ (x_P - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - (x_P + r)^2 ] / (2y_A).

Let's attempt coordinate approach.

Set M=(0,0), N=(d,0) with d>0. r < R, and circles intersect: condition: |R-r| < d < R+r. Also we have r < R, so R-r >0. So d > R-r.

Define:

C = (-r, 0)
D = (d+R, 0)

A = (x_A, y_A) with y_A > 0, where x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d), y_A = sqrt(r^2 - x_A^2). Also B = (x_A, -y_A).

Now P is circumcenter of A, C, D. Since C and D on x-axis, we have x_P = (C_x + D_x)/2 = (-r + d+R)/2 = (d+R-r)/2. Let's denote x_P = (d+R-r)/2.

Now y_P from condition PA = PC (or PA = PD). Use PC^2 = (x_P - (-r))^2 + (y_P - 0)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.
PA^2 = (x_P - x_A)^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2.
Set equal:

(x_P - x_A)^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

Expand: (x_P - x_A)^2 + y_P^2 - 2 y_P y_A + y_A^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.
Cancel y_P^2: (x_P - x_A)^2 - 2 y_P y_A + y_A^2 = (x_P + r)^2.
=> -2 y_P y_A = (x_P + r)^2 - (x_P - x_A)^2 - y_A^2.
=> 2 y_P y_A = (x_P - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - (x_P + r)^2.
=> y_P = [ (x_P - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - (x_P + r)^2 ] / (2 y_A).

Now we can simplify using known expressions.

Let’s compute x_P - x_A and x_P + r.

x_P = (d+R-r)/2.
x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d).

Compute x_P - x_A = (d+R-r)/2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d) = (1/2)[ (d+R-r) - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/d ] = (1/2)[ (d+R-r) - d - (r^2 - R^2)/d ]? Actually (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/d = d + (r^2 - R^2)/d. So:

x_P - x_A = (1/2)[ d+R-r - d - (r^2 - R^2)/d ] = (1/2)[ R - r - (r^2 - R^2)/d ] = (1/2)[ R - r - (r^2 - R^2)/d ].

But r^2 - R^2 = -(R^2 - r^2). So (r^2 - R^2)/d = -(R^2 - r^2)/d. So:

x_P - x_A = (1/2)[ R - r + (R^2 - r^2)/d ] = (1/2)[ R - r + (R - r)(R + r)/d ] = (1/2)(R - r)[1 + (R+r)/d].

Thus x_P - x_A = (R - r)/2 * (1 + (R+r)/d) = (R - r)/2 * ((d + R + r)/d) = (R - r)(d + R + r)/(2d).

Now x_P + r = (d+R-r)/2 + r = (d+R-r + 2r)/2 = (d+R+r)/2.

So (x_P + r)^2 = (d+R+r)^2/4.

Now y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2. We have x_A^2 = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2/(4d^2). So y_A^2 = r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2/(4d^2). This is a known expression: y_A^2 = (4d^2 r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2)/(4d^2). The numerator is (2dr - (d^2+r^2-R^2))(2dr + (d^2+r^2-R^2)) = ((R^2 - (d-r)^2) * ((d+r)^2 - R^2))? Actually standard: For two intersecting circles, the distance from the line of centers to the intersection points is given by (1/(2d)) * sqrt(( (R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2 )). So y_A = (1/(2d)) * sqrt(( (R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2 )). So y_A^2 = (( (R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2 ))/(4d^2). That's a nicer expression.

Now compute (x_P - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - (x_P + r)^2.

We have (x_P - x_A)^2 = ((R - r)^2 (d+R+r)^2)/(4d^2). And y_A^2 = (( (R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2 ))/(4d^2). And (x_P + r)^2 = (d+R+r)^2/4 = (d+R+r)^2/4.

So numerator (without denominator 4d^2) for (x_P - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 is:

(R - r)^2 (d+R+r)^2 + ((R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2). Then we subtract (x_P+r)^2 which is (d+R+r)^2/4, but we need common denominator 4d^2? Actually we want to compute:

(x_P - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - (x_P + r)^2 = [ (R - r)^2 (d+R+r)^2 + ((R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2) ]/(4d^2) - (d+R+r)^2/4.

Put over denominator 4d^2: = [ (R - r)^2 (d+R+r)^2 + ((R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2) - d^2 (d+R+r)^2 ]/(4d^2).

So numerator N = (R - r)^2 (d+R+r)^2 + ((R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2) - d^2 (d+R+r)^2.

We can try to simplify.

Let’s denote S = d+R+r. Then (R - r)^2 S^2 + ((R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2) - d^2 S^2 = S^2[(R - r)^2 - d^2] + ((R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2).

But note that (R - r)^2 - d^2 = -(d^2 - (R-r)^2). So S^2[(R - r)^2 - d^2] = -S^2 (d^2 - (R-r)^2). Then N = -S^2 (d^2 - (R-r)^2) + ((R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2) = (d^2 - (R-r)^2)[ ((R+r)^2 - d^2) - S^2 ].

Now S = d+R+r, so S^2 = (d+R+r)^2 = d^2 + (R+r)^2 + 2d(R+r). Then ((R+r)^2 - d^2) - S^2 = (R+r)^2 - d^2 - d^2 - (R+r)^2 - 2d(R+r) = -2d^2 - 2d(R+r) = -2d(d+R+r) = -2d S.

Thus N = (d^2 - (R-r)^2) * (-2d S) = -2d S (d^2 - (R-r)^2).

Therefore:

(x_P - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - (x_P + r)^2 = N/(4d^2) = -2d S (d^2 - (R-r)^2) / (4d^2) = - (S (d^2 - (R-r)^2)) / (2d).

So y_P = [ (x_P - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - (x_P + r)^2 ] / (2 y_A) = [ - (S (d^2 - (R-r)^2)) / (2d) ] / (2 y_A) = - (S (d^2 - (R-r)^2)) / (4d y_A).

Thus y_P = - ( (d+R+r) (d^2 - (R-r)^2) ) / (4d y_A).

Since y_A > 0, y_P is negative. So P is below the x-axis? Actually y_P negative, so P lies below MN (since MN is x-axis). That makes sense because A is above, and C and D on x-axis, the circumcenter of ACD might be below if A is above and CD is horizontal. But we can check: For triangle with base CD on x-axis and vertex A above, the circumcenter lies on perpendicular bisector of CD (vertical line) and its y-coordinate is determined by the perpendicular bisector of AC. Typically, if A is above, the circumcenter could be above or below depending on shape. Here we got negative, so P is below.

Now we have P = (x_P, y_P) with x_P = (d+R-r)/2, y_P = - ( (d+R+r) (d^2 - (R-r)^2) ) / (4d y_A). But note that d^2 - (R-r)^2 = (d - (R-r))(d + (R-r)). Since circles intersect, d > R-r, so positive.

Now we also have A = (x_A, y_A). So line AP: we can compute its slope. Since we need line through H parallel to AP, we need direction of AP.

But perhaps we can find coordinates of H (orthocenter of triangle PMN). M=(0,0), N=(d,0), P=(x_P, y_P). So triangle PMN has base MN on x-axis. The orthocenter H is the intersection of altitudes. Since M and N are on x-axis, the altitude from P is vertical line through P? Actually altitude from P is perpendicular to MN, and MN is horizontal, so altitude from P is vertical line x = x_P. The altitude from M is line through M perpendicular to PN. Similarly altitude from N is line through N perpendicular to PM. Orthocenter H lies on altitude from P, so H has x-coordinate x_P. Also H lies on altitude from M (or N). So we can find y-coordinate of H by intersection of x = x_P with altitude from M.

Thus H = (x_P, y_H) where y_H is such that line MH is perpendicular to PN.

Compute vector PN = N - P = (d - x_P, -y_P). So direction of PN = (d - x_P, -y_P). Altitude from M is line through M=(0,0) with direction perpendicular to PN, i.e., direction vector = (y_P, d - x_P) (since dot product (d-x_P, -y_P)·(y_P, d-x_P) = (d-x_P)y_P + (-y_P)(d-x_P)=0). So altitude from M is line: (x,y) = t (y_P, d - x_P). Its equation: y = ((d - x_P)/y_P) x, provided y_P ≠ 0. Since y_P is negative, non-zero.

Now H lies on this line and also on x = x_P. So plug x = x_P: y_H = ((d - x_P)/y_P) x_P.

Thus y_H = x_P (d - x_P) / y_P.

Now x_P = (d+R-r)/2. Then d - x_P = d - (d+R-r)/2 = (2d - d - R + r)/2 = (d - R + r)/2.

So x_P (d - x_P) = ((d+R-r)/2)*((d - R + r)/2) = ((d+R-r)(d - R + r))/4 = ( (d + (R-r)) (d - (R-r)) )/4 = (d^2 - (R-r)^2)/4.

Thus y_H = (d^2 - (R-r)^2)/4) / y_P = (d^2 - (R-r)^2) / (4 y_P).

Now y_P = - ( (d+R+r) (d^2 - (R-r)^2) ) / (4d y_A). So:

y_H = (d^2 - (R-r)^2) / (4 * ( - ( (d+R+r) (d^2 - (R-r)^2) / (4d y_A) ) = (d^2 - (R-r)^2) * (4d y_A) / (4 * ( - (d+R+r) (d^2 - (R-r)^2) ) = (d^2 - (R-r)^2) * (4d y_A) / ( -4 (d+R+r) (d^2 - (R-r)^2) ) = (4d y_A) / ( -4 (d+R+r) ) = - (d y_A) / (d+R+r).

Thus y_H = - (d y_A) / (d+R+r). So H = (x_P, y_H) = ( (d+R-r)/2, - (d y_A)/(d+R+r) ).

Now note that y_H is negative, as expected.

Now we have line AP: passes through A(x_A, y_A) and P(x_P, y_P). Its direction vector v = P - A = (x_P - x_A, y_P - y_A). We already computed x_P - x_A = (R - r)(d+R+r)/(2d) = (R - r)S/(2d) where S = d+R+r. And y_P - y_A = - (S (d^2 - (R-r)^2))/(4d y_A) - y_A = -(S (d^2 - (R-r)^2))/(4d y_A) - y_A = -(1/(4d y_A))[ S (d^2 - (R-r)^2) + 4d y_A^2 ].

But we can also compute using known relations: Since y_A^2 = (( (R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2 ))/(4d^2). So 4d y_A^2 = ( ( (R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2 ) )/(d). Actually 4d y_A^2 = 4d * ( ((R+r)^2-d^2)(d^2-(R-r)^2) )/(4d^2) = ( ((R+r)^2-d^2)(d^2-(R-r)^2) )/d. So then S (d^2-(R-r)^2) + 4d y_A^2 = S (d^2-(R-r)^2) + ( ((R+r)^2-d^2)(d^2-(R-r)^2) )/d = (d^2-(R-r)^2)[ S + ((R+r)^2-d^2)/d ] = (d^2-(R-r)^2)[ (d+R+r) + ((R+r)^2-d^2)/d ] = (d^2-(R-r)^2)[ (d+R+r) + ((R+r-d)(R+r+d))/d ] = (d^2-(R-r)^2)[ (d+R+r) + ((R+r-d)(R+r+d))/d ].

But maybe we don't need explicit slope; we can find the line through H parallel to AP: it has same direction as AP. So its equation can be written as: (x - x_P, y - y_H) is proportional to v = (x_P - x_A, y_P - y_A). So we need to show that this line is tangent to circle (BEF). E is second intersection of line AP with Ω (circle centered at M radius r). Since A is on Ω, and line AP intersects Ω at A and E. So we can find E by using the fact that A and E are symmetric with respect to the line through M perpendicular to AP? Actually not exactly. For a circle, if we have a line through a point A on the circle, the second intersection E can be found by reflecting A across the line through M perpendicular to AP? No, the chord AP: the line through M perpendicular to AP passes through the midpoint of chord AE. So we can find E by using that M is the center, and the line through M perpendicular to AP meets AE at its midpoint. So we can compute coordinates of E.

Similarly, F is second intersection of line AP with Γ (center N radius R). So we can compute F.

We have line AP: parametric: A + t * v, where v = P - A. Since A is on both circles, we can find t such that point lies on circle again. For Ω: |A + t v|^2 = r^2. Since |A|^2 = r^2, we have:

|A + t v|^2 = |A|^2 + 2 t A·v + t^2 |v|^2 = r^2 + 2 t A·v + t^2 |v|^2 = r^2 => 2 t A·v + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 => t (2 A·v + t |v|^2) = 0. t=0 gives A. So the other t satisfies t = -2 (A·v) / |v|^2. Thus E = A + t_E v, where t_E = -2 (A·v)/|v|^2.

Similarly, for Γ: |A + t v - N|^2 = R^2. Since |A - N|^2 = R^2, we have:

|A - N + t v|^2 = |A-N|^2 + 2 t (A-N)·v + t^2 |v|^2 = R^2 + 2 t (A-N)·v + t^2 |v|^2 = R^2 => 2 t (A-N)·v + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 => t (2 (A-N)·v + t |v|^2) = 0. So t_F = -2 ((A-N)·v)/|v|^2.

Thus we can compute E and F coordinates.

Now we need to prove that line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF. So we need to show that the distance from the center of circle (BEF) to that line equals its radius. Alternatively, we can show that the line through H parallel to AP is the radical axis of two circles? Or that H has equal power with respect to circle (BEF) and something? Another synthetic approach: Show that H lies on the polar of A with respect to circle (BEF)? Not sure.

Given the algebraic nature, we can compute the circle (BEF) equation and then verify tangency condition. That might be heavy but doable with computer algebra; however, we need to produce a human-readable proof. But we can attempt to simplify using known properties: maybe the circumcircle of BEF is the circle with diameter something, or maybe it's the circle with center at the midpoint of something. Let's try to compute coordinates of E and F and see if there is a pattern.

We have A = (x_A, y_A). v = P - A = (x_P - x_A, y_P - y_A). We computed x_P - x_A = (R - r)S/(2d). Let's denote Δx = (R - r)S/(2d). And Δy = y_P - y_A. We have y_P = - (S (d^2 - (R-r)^2))/(4d y_A). So Δy = - (S (d^2 - (R-r)^2))/(4d y_A) - y_A = - (S (d^2 - (R-r)^2) + 4d y_A^2) / (4d y_A). Let's compute numerator: S (d^2 - (R-r)^2) + 4d y_A^2. Using y_A^2 = (( (R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2 ))/(4d^2). So 4d y_A^2 = ( ((R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2 ))/d. So numerator = S (d^2 - (R-r)^2) + (( (R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2 ))/d = (d^2 - (R-r)^2)[ S + ((R+r)^2 - d^2)/d ] = (d^2 - (R-r)^2)[ (d+R+r) + ((R+r)^2 - d^2)/d ].

Now ((R+r)^2 - d^2) = (R+r-d)(R+r+d). So ((R+r)^2 - d^2)/d = (R+r-d)(R+r+d)/d. Then S + ((R+r)^2 - d^2)/d = (d+R+r) + (R+r-d)(R+r+d)/d = (d+R+r) + ((R+r-d)(R+r+d))/d.

Let’s compute common denominator d: = (d(d+R+r) + (R+r-d)(R+r+d))/d = (d^2 + d(R+r) + (R+r-d)(R+r+d))/d. Expand (R+r-d)(R+r+d) = (R+r)^2 - d^2 = (R+r)^2 - d^2. So numerator: d^2 + d(R+r) + (R+r)^2 - d^2 = d(R+r) + (R+r)^2 = (R+r)(d + R+r) = (R+r) S. So S + ((R+r)^2 - d^2)/d = (R+r) S / d.

Thus numerator = (d^2 - (R-r)^2) * ( (R+r) S / d ) = (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)S/d.

Therefore Δy = - [ (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)S/d ] / (4d y_A) = - (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)S) / (4d^2 y_A).

Thus v = (Δx, Δy) = ( (R - r)S/(2d), - (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)S/(4d^2 y_A) ).

We can factor S/(2d) perhaps: Δx = (S/(2d)) (R - r). Δy = - (S/(2d)) * ( (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r) / (2d y_A) )? Actually (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)S/(4d^2 y_A) = (S/(2d)) * ( (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r) / (2d y_A) . So v = (S/(2d)) * ( (R - r), - (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)/(2d y_A) ) ). But maybe we don't need explicit.

Now compute A·v. A = (x_A, y_A). x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). We'll need A·v = x_A Δx + y_A Δy.

Compute x_A Δx = x_A * (R - r)S/(2d) = (R - r)S/(2d) * x_A.

y_A Δy = y_A * [ - (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)S/(4d^2 y_A) ] = - (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)S/(4d^2).

Thus A·v = (R - r)S/(2d) x_A - (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)S/(4d^2).

Now x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). So (R - r)S/(2d) * x_A = (R - r)S/(2d) * (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d) = (R - r)S (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (4d^2).

Thus A·v = S/(4d^2) [ (R - r)(d^2 + r^2 - R^2) - (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r) ].

Simplify the bracket: (R - r)(d^2 + r^2 - R^2) - (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r). Note that d^2 - (R-r)^2 = (d - (R-r))(d + (R-r)). Expand both terms.

Compute (R - r)(d^2 + r^2 - R^2) = (R - r)d^2 + (R - r)(r^2 - R^2) = (R - r)d^2 + (R - r)(r - R)(r + R) = (R - r)d^2 - (R - r)^2 (R + r). Because (R - r)(r^2 - R^2) = (R - r)(r - R)(r + R) = -(R - r)^2 (R + r). So first term = (R - r)d^2 - (R - r)^2 (R + r).

Second term: (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r) = (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r) = (R+r)d^2 - (R+r)(R-r)^2.

Thus bracket = [ (R - r)d^2 - (R - r)^2 (R + r) ] - [ (R+r)d^2 - (R+r)(R-r)^2 ] = (R - r)d^2 - (R - r)^2 (R+r) - (R+r)d^2 + (R+r)(R-r)^2 = (R - r - R - r)d^2 + [ - (R - r)^2 (R+r) + (R+r)(R-r)^2 ] = (-2r)d^2 + 0 = -2r d^2.

Thus A·v = S/(4d^2) * (-2r d^2) = - (r S)/2.

So A·v = - (r S)/2.

Now |v|^2 = Δx^2 + Δy^2. Compute Δx^2 = (R - r)^2 S^2/(4d^2). Δy^2 = (d^2 - (R-r)^2)^2 (R+r)^2 S^2/(16 d^4 y_A^2). But y_A^2 = (( (R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2 ))/(4d^2). So 1/y_A^2 = 4d^2/(( (R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2 )). Then Δy^2 = (d^2 - (R-r)^2)^2 (R+r)^2 S^2/(16 d^4) * (4d^2/(( (R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2 )) = (d^2 - (R-r)^2)^2 (R+r)^2 S^2 * 4d^2 / (16 d^4 ( (R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2 )) = (d^2 - (R-r)^2) (R+r)^2 S^2 / (4 d^2 ( (R+r)^2 - d^2 )). So |v|^2 = (R - r)^2 S^2/(4d^2) + (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)^2 S^2/(4 d^2 ( (R+r)^2 - d^2 )) = S^2/(4d^2) [ (R - r)^2 + (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)^2/((R+r)^2 - d^2) ].

Now note that ((R+r)^2 - d^2) = (R+r-d)(R+r+d). And (d^2 - (R-r)^2) = (d - (R-r))(d + (R-r)). So the second term is (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)^2/((R+r)^2 - d^2). This is reminiscent of something. Let’s denote U = R+r, V = R-r. Then d^2 - V^2 = (d-V)(d+V), U^2 - d^2 = (U-d)(U+d). Then expression: V^2 + (d^2 - V^2) U^2/(U^2 - d^2) = V^2 + (d^2 - V^2) U^2/(U^2 - d^2). Combine over common denominator (U^2 - d^2): = [V^2 (U^2 - d^2) + (d^2 - V^2) U^2] / (U^2 - d^2) = [V^2 U^2 - V^2 d^2 + d^2 U^2 - V^2 U^2] / (U^2 - d^2) = [ - V^2 d^2 + d^2 U^2 ] / (U^2 - d^2) = d^2 (U^2 - V^2) / (U^2 - d^2). But U^2 - V^2 = (R+r)^2 - (R-r)^2 = 4Rr. So expression = d^2 * 4Rr / (U^2 - d^2) = 4Rr d^2 / ((R+r)^2 - d^2). Thus |v|^2 = S^2/(4d^2) * (4Rr d^2/((R+r)^2 - d^2)) = S^2 * Rr / ((R+r)^2 - d^2). So |v|^2 = Rr S^2 / ((R+r)^2 - d^2). Good.

Now t_E = -2 (A·v)/|v|^2 = -2 * (-r S/2) / |v|^2 = (r S) / |v|^2 = r S / (Rr S^2/((R+r)^2 - d^2)) = r S * ((R+r)^2 - d^2) / (Rr S^2) = ((R+r)^2 - d^2) / (R S). So t_E = ((R+r)^2 - d^2) / (R S). Then E = A + t_E v.

Similarly, t_F = -2 ((A-N)·v)/|v|^2. Compute (A-N)·v = (x_A - d, y_A)·v = (x_A - d)Δx + y_A Δy. We have x_A - d = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d) - d = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2 - 2d^2)/(2d) = (-d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d) = -(d^2 + R^2 - r^2)/(2d). So (x_A - d) = -(d^2 + R^2 - r^2)/(2d). Then (x_A - d)Δx = -(d^2+R^2-r^2)/(2d) * (R - r)S/(2d) = -(R - r)S (d^2+R^2-r^2)/(4d^2). And y_A Δy = y_A * [ - (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)S/(4d^2 y_A) ] = - (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)S/(4d^2). So (A-N)·v = -(R - r)S (d^2+R^2-r^2)/(4d^2) - (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)S/(4d^2) = -S/(4d^2)[ (R - r)(d^2+R^2-r^2) + (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r) ].

Simplify bracket: (R - r)(d^2+R^2-r^2) + (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r). Expand: (R - r)d^2 + (R - r)(R^2 - r^2) + (R+r)d^2 - (R+r)(R-r)^2 = (R - r)d^2 + (R - r)(R - r)(R+r) + (R+r)d^2 - (R+r)(R-r)^2 = (R - r)d^2 + (R - r)^2 (R+r) + (R+r)d^2 - (R+r)(R-r)^2 = (R - r + R + r)d^2 + [ (R - r)^2 (R+r) - (R+r)(R-r)^2 ] = (2R)d^2 + 0 = 2R d^2. So bracket = 2R d^2. Thus (A-N)·v = -S/(4d^2) * 2R d^2 = - (R S)/2.

So (A-N)·v = - (R S)/2. Then t_F = -2 * (-R S/2) / |v|^2 = (R S) / |v|^2 = R S / (Rr S^2/((R+r)^2 - d^2)) = R S * ((R+r)^2 - d^2) / (Rr S^2) = ((R+r)^2 - d^2) / (r S). So t_F = ((R+r)^2 - d^2) / (r S). Note that t_E = ((R+r)^2 - d^2) / (R S). So t_F = (R/r) t_E? Actually t_F = ((R+r)^2 - d^2)/(r S) = (R/r) * ((R+r)^2 - d^2)/(R S) = (R/r) t_E. So t_F = (R/r) t_E.

Thus we have E and F in terms of A and v.

Now we also have B = (x_A, -y_A). So we have coordinates of B, E, F. We need to show that line through H parallel to AP (i.e., direction v) is tangent to circle (BEF). That is, the line L: (x,y) = H + s v (s real) is tangent to circle through B, E, F.

We can try to compute the equation of circle (BEF) and then compute distance from its center to L, or compute power of point H with respect to that circle? Actually tangency condition: line L is tangent to circle (BEF) if and only if the distance from the center of circle (BEF) to L equals the radius. But we can also use that if we take any point X on L, the power of X with respect to circle (BEF) is (distance from X to center)^2 - radius^2. For tangency, there is exactly one intersection, so the quadratic equation for intersection has discriminant zero. Alternatively, we can show that H lies on the polar of some point with respect to circle (BEF). Since L is through H and parallel to AP, perhaps H is the orthocenter of PMN, and there is a known relation: the line through H parallel to AP is the Simson line of some point? Not sure.

Thus the algebraic approach works. Now we need to produce a rigorous proof, possibly synthetic or analytic. Since we have derived expressions, we can present a coordinate proof. However, we must be careful: The problem is from geometry olympiad, and a coordinate proof is acceptable if done correctly. But we should also consider degenerate cases (like when y_A=0? But circles intersect at two distinct points, so y_A>0, non-zero). Also r<R, so S positive.

We can present a clean analytic proof: Place coordinates as described, compute coordinates of A, B, C, D, P, H, E, F, then find equation of line through H parallel to AP, and find equation of circle (BEF), then verify that the distance from the center of that circle to the line equals its radius. That would be a valid proof. However, we need to present it in a way that is clear and not overly messy. We can simplify using algebraic identities.

Alternatively, we can try to find a synthetic proof. Let's explore synthetic possibilities.

We have circles Ω and Γ intersecting at A, B. Let O1 = M, O2 = N. Line O1O2 meets circles at C and D. P is circumcenter of ACD. So P is the center of the circle through A, C, D. Since C and D are on O1O2, the circle (ACD) is orthogonal to something? Possibly the radical axis.

Note that A, C, D, and B? Not.

Observe that P is the midpoint of the arc CD of circle (ACD) that contains A? Actually since C and D are endpoints of a chord, the perpendicular bisector of CD passes through the center P. So P is on the perpendicular bisector of CD. Also note that M and N are on CD. So we have a right angle? Possibly we can show that AP is the symmedian in triangle PMN or something.

Let's try to find relationships: In triangle PMN, we have P, M, N. Since M and N are on line CD, and P is on perpendicular bisector of CD, we have that PM and PN are not necessarily equal. But we can compute angles.

Maybe we can use the fact that H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. So H is the point such that PH ⟂ MN, MH ⟂ PN, NH ⟂ PM. Since MN is horizontal, PH is vertical. So H lies on the line through P perpendicular to MN. Also, AP is a line through A and P. So line through H parallel to AP is the line through H with direction AP. We need to show this line is tangent to circle (BEF). So we need to show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circle through B, E, F.

Now note that B, E, F are points on Ω and Γ. Perhaps we can show that the circle (BEF) is the circle with diameter the projection of H onto something? Or maybe it's the circle with center at the midpoint of something like the midpoint of the arc? Let's compute in our numeric: Center O = (2.5,-2.5) which is the midpoint of something? B=(1.8,-2.4), E=(2.4,-1.8), F=(2.6,-3.2). The center (2.5,-2.5) is the average of B and something? Actually (1.8+2.6)/2=2.2, not 2.5. But maybe it's the midpoint of the line joining the centers of the two circles? M=(0,0), N=(5,0), midpoint is (2.5,0). Not (2.5,-2.5). So not.

Alternatively, note that in the coordinate proof, we found that the circle (BEF) has center (5/2, -5/2) and radius √2/2. That center is the point (5/2, -5/2). Interestingly, that point is the midpoint of the segment joining the points (5/2,0) and (5/2,-5)? Not.

Observe that the line L: y = -7x+20. The distance from O to L is 1/√2. And O is (5/2, -5/2). So O lies on the line through H? H=(3,-1). O-H = (-0.5, -1.5). Not perpendicular.

Maybe there is a known configuration: The circumcircle of BEF is the circle with diameter the midpoint of something? Actually we can compute O in terms of d, r, R. Let's do that.

We have general expressions:

x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d)
y_A = (1/(2d)) * sqrt(( (R+r)^2 - d^2)( d^2 - (R-r)^2 ))

But we can use the fact that A lies on both circles. We have coordinates for A, B, C, D, P, H, E, F.

We derived:

P = (x_P, y_P) where x_P = (d+R-r)/2, y_P = - (S (d^2 - V^2))/(4d y_A) where S = d+R+r, V = R-r. But we also found y_H = - (d y_A)/S, and H = (x_P, y_H).

We also found E = A + t_E v, with t_E = ((R+r)^2 - d^2)/(R S). F = A + t_F v, with t_F = ((R+r)^2 - d^2)/(r S). And v = P - A.

We can use these relations to simplify expressions.

So (U^2-d^2)(d^2-V^2) = 4d^2 y_A^2. Therefore t_E Δy = - (4d^2 y_A^2) U/(4d^2 y_A R S) = - (y_A U)/(R S). So t_E Δy = - (y_A U)/(R S). Similarly, t_F Δy = - (y_A U)/(r S). Good!

Thus we have:

E = (x_A + (U^2-d^2)V/(2dR), y_A - (y_A U)/(R S) ) = (x_A + (U^2-d^2)V/(2dR), y_A (1 - U/(R S)) ).

Given the algebraic complexity, but we have already derived that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF) in our numeric. So we can proceed with analytic proof. To make it rigorous and clear, we can compute the equation of the circle (BEF) and then compute the distance from its center to line L, and show it equals radius. That is straightforward, though algebraically heavy. But we can simplify using the derived expressions.

Let's attempt to compute the circle (BEF) center O = (X0, Y0) and radius r_c. We have B, E, F coordinates. Instead of solving for A,B,C, we can compute the circumcenter directly using perpendicular bisectors. Since we have coordinates, we can compute the midpoint and slope of BE and BF, then find intersection.

Let's compute BE and BF.

B = (x_A, -y_A)
E = (x_A + α/R, y_A (1 - U/(RS))) = (x_A + α/R, y_A - (y_A U)/(R S))
F = (x_A + α/r, y_A - (y_A U)/(r S))

We can denote:

E_x = x_A + α/R
E_y = y_A - (y_A U)/(R S) = y_A (1 - U/(RS))
F_x = x_A + α/r
F_y = y_A (1 - U/(rS))

Now compute the perpendicular bisector of BE. Midpoint M_BE = ((x_A + E_x)/2, (-y_A + E_y)/2) = ((2x_A + α/R)/2, (-y_A + y_A - (y_A U)/(RS))/2) = (x_A + α/(2R), - (y_A U)/(2RS)). So M_BE = (x_A + α/(2R), - (y_A U)/(2RS)). Slope of BE: (E_y - (-y_A))/(E_x - x_A) = (y_A - (y_A U)/(RS) + y_A)/(α/R) = (2y_A - (y_A U)/(RS))/(α/R) = y_A (2 - U/(RS)) * (R/α). So slope m_BE = y_A R (2 - U/(RS))/α. Then perpendicular slope = -1/m_BE = -α/(y_A R (2 - U/(RS))). So the equation of perpendicular bisector of BE is: y - M_BE_y = m_perp (x - M_BE_x). That line passes through circumcenter O.

Similarly, for BF: midpoint M_BF = ((x_A + F_x)/2, (-y_A + F_y)/2) = (x_A + α/(2r), - (y_A U)/(2rS)). Slope of BF: (F_y - (-y_A))/(F_x - x_A) = (y_A - (y_A U)/(rS) + y_A)/(α/r) = y_A (2 - U/(rS)) * (r/α). So m_BF = y_A r (2 - U/(rS))/α. Perpendicular slope = -α/(y_A r (2 - U/(rS))).

Now O is intersection of these two perpendicular bisectors. So we can solve for O.

Let’s denote:

O = (X, Y). Then:

Y - (- (y_A U)/(2RS)) = -α/(y_A R (2 - U/(RS))) (X - (x_A + α/(2R)))   (eq1)
Y - (- (y_A U)/(2rS)) = -α/(y_A r (2 - U/(rS))) (X - (x_A + α/(2r)))   (eq2)

We can solve for X and Y. This looks messy but maybe simplifies.

Alternatively, we can compute the circumcircle by using the fact that the circle (BEF) is the circle through B, E, F. Since we have expressions for E and F in terms of x_A,y_A and the parameters, we can try to find the equation of the circle in a more systematic way: For any point (x,y) on the circle, we have that the power with respect to Ω and Γ might be related. Another approach: Use the concept of radical axis: The circle (BEF) and Ω intersect at B and E. So the line BE is the radical axis of Ω and (BEF). Similarly, BF is the radical axis of Γ and (BEF). So we can find the equation of (BEF) by using that its radical axis with Ω is line BE, and with Γ is line BF. That might give a linear equation for the circle's equation.

Given a circle (x^2+y^2+Ax+By+C=0), its radical axis with Ω: x^2+y^2 - r^2 = 0 is: (x^2+y^2+Ax+By+C) - (x^2+y^2 - r^2) = 0 => Ax+By+C+r^2=0. So the radical axis of (BEF) and Ω is the line Ax+By+C+r^2=0. But we know that this line passes through B and E (since they are intersection points). So we have that the line through B and E is given by Ax+By+C+r^2=0. Similarly, the radical axis of (BEF) and Γ: (x-d)^2+y^2 - R^2 = 0 gives: (x^2+y^2+Ax+By+C) - ((x-d)^2+y^2 - R^2) = 0 => Ax+By+C - (x^2+y^2 -2dx+d^2 - R^2) =0? Actually compute: (x^2+y^2+Ax+By+C) - ((x-d)^2+y^2 - R^2) = Ax+By+C - (x^2-2dx+d^2+y^2 - R^2) = Ax+By+C - x^2 +2dx - d^2 - y^2 + R^2 = -x^2 - y^2 + (A+2d)x + By + (C - d^2 + R^2). But that is not linear because of -x^2-y^2. So the radical axis of two circles is given by the difference of their equations, which is linear. The equation of Γ: (x-d)^2+y^2 - R^2 = x^2+y^2 -2dx + d^2 - R^2 = 0. So the radical axis of (BEF) and Γ is: (x^2+y^2+Ax+By+C) - (x^2+y^2 -2dx + d^2 - R^2) = 0 => (A+2d)x + By + (C - d^2 + R^2) = 0. So that is linear. So we have two linear equations that define the radical axes. Since the radical axis of (BEF) and Ω is line BE, we have that the line BE is given by: Ax+By+C+r^2=0. Similarly, the line BF is given by: (A+2d)x + By + (C - d^2 + R^2)=0.

Thus we can find A, B, C from these two linear equations and the fact that the circle passes through B (or E,F). Actually, we have three unknowns A,B,C. We can use that the line BE is given by Ax+By+C+r^2=0, and that line BF is given by (A+2d)x+By+C-d^2+R^2=0. But we also know that B lies on both lines (since B is intersection of Ω and Γ, and also on (BEF) obviously). So we can plug B into these equations to get relations. However, we can also find the circle equation by using that the radical axis with Ω is line BE, and that the circle passes through B and E. But we need to find A,B,C.

We can use the fact that the line BE is the radical axis of (BEF) and Ω. So the equation of line BE is: Ax+By+C+r^2=0. Similarly, line BF: (A+2d)x+By+C-d^2+R^2=0. Let's compute the equation of line BE. Points: B(x_A, -y_A), E(x_A+α/R, y_A - (y_A U)/(RS)). Alternatively, we can find the equation of line BE in the form: L1(x,y)=0. Then we have that L1(x,y) = Ax+By+C+r^2. So we can compute A,B,C from that. Not exactly; the radical axis condition gives that the line BE is the set of points where powers are equal. Let's compute the line BE equation explicitly in terms of x_A,y_A,α,R,S,U.

We have m_BE = y_A R (2 - U/(RS))/α. So line BE: y + y_A = (y_A R (2 - U/(RS))/α) (x - x_A). Multiply both sides by α: α(y + y_A) = y_A R (2 - U/(RS)) (x - x_A). So:

α y + α y_A = y_A R (2 - U/(RS)) x - y_A R (2 - U/(RS)) x_A.

Bring all terms: y_A R (2 - U/(RS)) x - α y - [α y_A + y_A R (2 - U/(RS)) x_A] = 0.

Thus line BE: y_A R (2 - U/(RS)) x - α y - [α y_A + y_A R (2 - U/(RS)) x_A] = 0.

B = -α   (coefficient of y)
C+r^2 = - [α y_A + y_A R (2 - U/(RS)) x_A]   (constant term)

A = y_A R (2 - U/(RS))
B = -α
C+r^2 = - [α y_A + y_A R (2 - U/(RS)) x_A] = -y_A [α + R (2 - U/(RS)) x_A].

So we have A = 3.0 from that, but we already have A = 7.4167. So inconsistency. Wait, the radical axis of (BEF) and Γ is indeed line BF because they intersect at B and F. So the equation of line BF must be the radical axis. So the coefficients we derived from the actual line BF should satisfy that (A+2d) is the coefficient of x in the radical axis equation. But we have two different expressions for A from the two lines. So either the circle (BEF) is not the same as the one we think? Actually, the circle (BEF) is defined by points B, E, F. So it is a specific circle. The radical axis with Ω is line BE, and with Γ is line BF. So the line BE and BF are the radical axes. So we can use the radical axis conditions to find the equation of the circle. That gives a linear system for A,B,C. Let's set up the equations properly.

We have circle: x^2+y^2+Ax+By+C=0. For Ω: x^2+y^2 - r^2 = 0. The radical axis is: (x^2+y^2+Ax+By+C) - (x^2+y^2 - r^2) = Ax+By+C+r^2=0. This line must pass through B and E. So we have:

Ax_B + By_B + C + r^2 = 0   (since B lies on radical axis)
Ax_E + By_E + C + r^2 = 0   (E lies on radical axis)

Similarly, for Γ: (x-d)^2+y^2 - R^2 = 0 => x^2+y^2 -2dx + d^2 - R^2 = 0. The radical axis is: (x^2+y^2+Ax+By+C) - (x^2+y^2 -2dx + d^2 - R^2) = (A+2d)x + By + (C - d^2 + R^2) = 0. This line must pass through B and F. So:

(A+2d)x_B + B y_B + (C - d^2 + R^2) = 0
(A+2d)x_F + B y_F + (C - d^2 + R^2) = 0

Thus we have four equations, but they are not independent; they define the circle. We can use these to solve for A,B,C. Since we know coordinates of B,E,F, we can compute A,B,C explicitly. That is a straightforward linear system. Let's do that.

Let’s denote:

B = (x_A, -y_A)
E = (x_A + α/R, y_A - (y_A U)/(R S)) = (x_A + α/R, y_A - (y_A U)/(R S))
F = (x_A + α/r, y_A - (y_A U)/(r S))

We can compute the circle equation by using the general formula for circumcircle. But perhaps we can find a simpler synthetic proof.

Given the algebraic approach, we can compute the circle (BEF) using the fact that it is the circle through B, E, F. We can compute its center O = (X0, Y0) and radius r_c. Then we need to show that the line L through H parallel to AP is tangent. That is, the distance from O to L equals r_c.

We have coordinates for H and v. So we can compute the distance from O to L using formula: distance = |(O-H) × v| / |v|. And we need to show that this equals r_c. So we need to compute O and r_c in terms of d,r,R. That might be doable with some algebra.

Let's attempt to compute O directly using the perpendicular bisector method but with our simplified expressions.

We have B = (x_A, -y_A)
E = (x_A + α/R, y_A - (y_A U)/(R S))
F = (x_A + α/r, y_A - (y_A U)/(r S))

We can also compute the circumcenter by solving the linear system for the circle equation. Let's do that.

Let’s denote the circle equation: x^2+y^2+Ax+By+C=0.

Plug B: x_A^2+y_A^2 + A x_A + B(-y_A) + C = 0 => r^2 + A x_A - B y_A + C = 0. (1)

Plug E: (x_A+α/R)^2 + (y_A - (y_A U)/(RS))^2 + A(x_A+α/R) + B(y_A - (y_A U)/(RS)) + C = 0.

Expand: x_A^2 + 2x_A α/R + (α/R)^2 + y_A^2 - 2y_A^2 U/(RS) + y_A^2 U^2/(R^2 S^2) + A x_A + A α/R + B y_A - B y_A U/(RS) + C = 0.

But x_A^2+y_A^2 = r^2. So:

r^2 + 2x_A α/R + (α/R)^2 - 2y_A^2 U/(RS) + y_A^2 U^2/(R^2 S^2) + A x_A + A α/R + B y_A - B y_A U/(RS) + C = 0.

Subtract (1): r^2 + A x_A - B y_A + C = 0. So we get:

2x_A α/R + (α/R)^2 - 2y_A^2 U/(RS) + y_A^2 U^2/(R^2 S^2) + A α/R - B y_A U/(RS) + B y_A = 0? Actually subtract: (E eq) - (B eq) gives:

(2x_A α/R + (α/R)^2 - 2y_A^2 U/(RS) + y_A^2 U^2/(R^2 S^2) + A α/R + B y_A - B y_A U/(RS)) - ( - B y_A ) = 0? Wait, we need to subtract the entire B eq: r^2 + A x_A - B y_A + C = 0. So subtract that from E eq: (r^2 + 2x_A α/R + (α/R)^2 - 2y_A^2 U/(RS) + y_A^2 U^2/(R^2 S^2) + A x_A + A α/R + B y_A - B y_A U/(RS) + C) - (r^2 + A x_A - B y_A + C) = 0 => 2x_A α/R + (α/R)^2 - 2y_A^2 U/(RS) + y_A^2 U^2/(R^2 S^2) + A α/R + B y_A - B y_A U/(RS) + B y_A = 0 => 2x_A α/R + (α/R)^2 - 2y_A^2 U/(RS) + y_A^2 U^2/(R^2 S^2) + A α/R + 2B y_A - B y_A U/(RS) = 0.

So:

A α/R + B y_A (2 - U/(RS)) = - [2x_A α/R + (α/R)^2 - 2y_A^2 U/(RS) + y_A^2 U^2/(R^2 S^2)].

Similarly, for F: we get:

A α/r + B y_A (2 - U/(rS)) = - [2x_A α/r + (α/r)^2 - 2y_A^2 U/(rS) + y_A^2 U^2/(r^2 S^2)].

Now we have two linear equations in A and B. We can solve for A and B. Then we can find C from (1). Then we can compute the circle's center O = (-A/2, -B/2) and radius r_c = sqrt(A^2/4+B^2/4-C). Then we need to verify that the line L: through H = (x_P, y_H) with direction v = (Δx, Δy) is tangent. That is, the distance from O to L equals r_c.

This is a lot of algebra, but we can attempt to simplify using the relations we have. Perhaps we can find that the circle (BEF) has a simple equation: (x - x_A)^2 + (y + y_A)^2 = something? Let's test with numeric: B=(1.8,-2.4), E=(2.4,-1.8), F=(2.6,-3.2). Compute (x-1.8)^2+(y+2.4)^2 for E: (0.6)^2+(-1.8+2.4)^2=0.36+0.36=0.72, radius squared =0.5? Not. For B: 0. For F: (0.8)^2+(-3.2+2.4)^2=0.64+0.64=1.28. So not.

Maybe the circle (BEF) is the circle with diameter the segment connecting the points (x_A, -y_A) and something? Not.

Given the complexity, but we have already derived that in the numeric, the line L is tangent. So we can try to compute the circle (BEF) in general using complex numbers or vector geometry. Another approach: Use inversion about A with radius sqrt(AB*AE?) Not.

Maybe we can find a synthetic proof: Show that H is the midpoint of the arc something, or that the line through H parallel to AP is the Simson line of A with respect to triangle BEF? But A is not on circle (BEF). However, if we consider the circle (BEF) and point A, the feet of perpendiculars from A to the sides of triangle BEF are collinear on the Simson line of A if A lies on the circumcircle. But A does not lie on that circle. So not.

Another synthetic idea: Use the fact that H is the orthocenter of triangle PMN. Then the line through H parallel to AP is the line through H that is perpendicular to the line joining the circumcenter of PMN to something? Not.

Maybe we can use the concept of the nine-point circle: The nine-point circle of triangle PMN passes through the midpoints of sides, feet of altitudes, and midpoints of segments from orthocenter to vertices. Since H is orthocenter, the line through H parallel to AP might be the line through H that is the reflection of something across the nine-point center? Not.

Given the algebraic approach, we can present a coordinate proof that is rigorous and not too messy if we use the derived expressions and simplify using the relations. Let's try to compute the circle (BEF) explicitly in terms of x_A,y_A,α,R,S,U, etc. Then compute the distance condition.

We have:

A = y_A R (2 - U/(RS)) from the first line condition? But that came from equating coefficients of x in line BE. However, we need to be careful: The line BE is given by Ax+By+C+r^2=0. But we also have that the line BE passes through B and E. So we can compute the equation of line BE directly, and then equate the coefficients to get A,B,C up to a common factor. However, the circle equation is determined up to a scaling factor? Actually, the circle equation x^2+y^2+Ax+By+C=0 is not homogeneous; the coefficients are fixed. So we cannot just set A equal to the coefficient of x in line BE because that line is Ax+By+C+r^2=0, and the coefficient of x is A, but we also have that the line BE is defined by its own equation. So if we compute the line BE in the form L1(x,y)=0, then we have that L1(x,y) = Ax+By+C+r^2. So the coefficients of L1 must be proportional to (A, B, C+r^2). But since the line equation is determined up to a multiplicative constant, we can choose the representation such that the coefficient of x in L1 is A, etc. So we can set A = coefficient of x in L1 (if we scale L1 appropriately). But we also have the line BF condition which gives another relation. So we can solve for A,B,C from these two linear equations and the fact that B lies on the circle. That is what we started.

Let's compute the line BE equation in the form: (y + y_A) = m_BE (x - x_A). Multiply both sides by something to get linear equation: y + y_A - m_BE (x - x_A) = 0 => -m_BE x + y + (y_A + m_BE x_A) = 0. So line BE: -m_BE x + y + (y_A + m_BE x_A) = 0. This is the equation. Then we have that this line is equal to Ax+By+C+r^2=0. So we have:

A = -m_BE
B = 1
C+r^2 = y_A + m_BE x_A

But careful: The line equation is: A x + B y + (C+r^2) = 0. So we have:

A = -m_BE
B = 1
C+r^2 = y_A + m_BE x_A

But is B=1? The coefficient of y in line BE is 1. So yes, B=1. But earlier we had B = -α from the line BF? That would be inconsistent. So we need to be careful: The line BE is not necessarily given by Ax+By+C+r^2=0 with the same A,B,C as the circle equation? Actually, the circle equation is x^2+y^2+Ax+By+C=0. The radical axis with Ω is: (x^2+y^2+Ax+By+C) - (x^2+y^2 - r^2) = Ax+By+C+r^2=0. So the line BE must be exactly the set of points where Ax+By+C+r^2=0. So if we write the line BE in the form L(x,y)=0, then L(x,y) = Ax+By+C+r^2, up to a non-zero constant factor. So the coefficients of L are proportional to (A, B, C+r^2). So we can set:

A = k * (coefficient of x in L)
B = k * (coefficient of y in L)
C+r^2 = k * (constant term in L)

for some non-zero k. So we have a scaling factor. Similarly, from line BF, we have:

A+2d = k' * (coefficient of x in BF)
B = k' * (coefficient of y in BF)
C - d^2 + R^2 = k' * (constant term in BF)

for some k'. So we have two different scaling factors. That's why we need to solve for A,B,C. So we cannot simply set A = -m_BE; we need to include the scaling factor.

Thus the proper approach is to set up the system:

Let the line BE be given by: L_BE(x,y)=0, where L_BE(x,y)= a_BE x + b_BE y + c_BE = 0. Then we have:

A = λ a_BE
B = λ b_BE
C+r^2 = λ c_BE

for some λ ≠ 0.

Similarly, for BF: L_BF(x,y)= a_BF x + b_BF y + c_BF = 0, we have:

A+2d = μ a_BF
B = μ b_BF
C - d^2 + R^2 = μ c_BF

for some μ ≠ 0.

We can solve for A,B,C,λ,μ. Since we have three unknowns A,B,C, and we have two linear equations from the line conditions (each gives two equations, but they involve λ and μ). Actually, we can eliminate λ and μ to get relations.

From the first set: A = λ a_BE, B = λ b_BE, C = λ c_BE - r^2.
From the second set: A = μ a_BF - 2d, B = μ b_BF, C = μ c_BF + d^2 - R^2.

Thus we have:

λ a_BE = μ a_BF - 2d   (1)
λ b_BE = μ b_BF        (2)
λ c_BE - r^2 = μ c_BF + d^2 - R^2   (3)

We can solve for λ and μ from (1) and (2) if a_BE and b_BE are not both zero. Then we can find A,B,C. So we need to compute a_BE, b_BE, c_BE from the coordinates of B and E. Similarly for BF.

Let's compute the line BE in the form a_BE x + b_BE y + c_BE = 0. Using points B(x_A, -y_A) and E(x_A+α/R, y_A - (y_A U)/(RS)). We can compute the equation by taking the determinant:

| x   y   1 |
| x_A -y_A 1 | = 0
| x_E  y_E 1 |

So the line equation is: (y_A - y_E) x + (x_E - x_A) y + (x_A y_E - x_E y_A) = 0. Actually, the determinant gives: (y_A - y_E)x + (x_E - x_A)y + (x_A y_E - x_E y_A)=0. So we have:

a_BE = y_A - y_E
b_BE = x_E - x_A
c_BE = x_A y_E - x_E y_A

Now compute these:

y_E = y_A - (y_A U)/(RS) = y_A (1 - U/(RS))
So y_A - y_E = y_A - y_A (1 - U/(RS)) = y_A * U/(RS).

x_E - x_A = α/R.

c_BE = x_A y_E - x_E y_A = x_A y_A (1 - U/(RS)) - (x_A + α/R) y_A = x_A y_A - x_A y_A U/(RS) - x_A y_A - (α/R) y_A = - y_A (x_A U/(RS) + α/R) = - y_A ( (x_A U)/(RS) + α/R ).

Thus line BE: (y_A U/(RS)) x + (α/R) y - y_A ( (x_A U)/(RS) + α/R ) = 0. Multiply both sides by RS to simplify: y_A U x + α S y - y_A ( x_A U + α S ) = 0? Actually, multiply by RS: (y_A U/(RS))*RS = y_A U, (α/R)*RS = α S, constant: - y_A ( (x_A U)/(RS) + α/R ) * RS = - y_A ( x_A U + α S ). So line BE: y_A U x + α S y - y_A ( x_A U + α S ) = 0. So we have:

a_BE = y_A U
b_BE = α S
c_BE = - y_A ( x_A U + α S )

But careful: The line equation is a_BE x + b_BE y + c_BE = 0. So we have:

a_BE = y_A U
b_BE = α S
c_BE = - y_A ( x_A U + α S )

Now the radical axis condition says: Ax+By+C+r^2 = λ (a_BE x + b_BE y + c_BE) for some λ. So:

A = λ y_A U
B = λ α S
C+r^2 = λ ( - y_A ( x_A U + α S ) )   (EqBE)

Similarly, for BF: points B(x_A,-y_A) and F(x_A+α/r, y_A - (y_A U)/(rS)). Compute line BF: (y_A - y_F) x + (x_F - x_A) y + (x_A y_F - x_F y_A) = 0.

y_F = y_A - (y_A U)/(rS) = y_A (1 - U/(rS))
So y_A - y_F = y_A * U/(rS).

x_F - x_A = α/r.

c_BF = x_A y_F - x_F y_A = x_A y_A (1 - U/(rS)) - (x_A + α/r) y_A = x_A y_A - x_A y_A U/(rS) - x_A y_A - (α/r) y_A = - y_A ( x_A U/(rS) + α/r ) = - y_A ( (x_A U)/(rS) + α/r ).

Thus line BF: (y_A U/(rS)) x + (α/r) y - y_A ( (x_A U)/(rS) + α/r ) = 0. Multiply by rS: y_A U x + α S y - y_A ( x_A U + α S ) = 0? Wait, multiply: (y_A U/(rS))*rS = y_A U, (α/r)*rS = α S, constant: - y_A ( (x_A U)/(rS) + α/r ) * rS = - y_A ( x_A U + α S ). So line BF: y_A U x + α S y - y_A ( x_A U + α S ) = 0. That is exactly the same as line BE! So both lines BE and BF have the same equation? That would mean B, E, F are collinear, which is not true in general. Let's check: In numeric, B=(1.8,-2.4), E=(2.4,-1.8), F=(2.6,-3.2). Are they collinear? Slope BE = ( -1.8+2.4)/(2.4-1.8)=0.6/0.6=1. Slope BF = ( -3.2+2.4)/(2.6-1.8)=(-0.8)/0.8=-1. So not collinear. So the line BF should be different. But our computation gave the same equation? That suggests an error in computing line BF. Let's recompute line BF carefully.

Points: B(x_A, -y_A). F(x_A+α/r, y_A - (y_A U)/(rS)). So x_F = x_A + α/r. y_F = y_A - (y_A U)/(rS). So y_F = y_A (1 - U/(rS)). Then y_A - y_F = y_A - y_A (1 - U/(rS)) = y_A * U/(rS). That's correct.

Now compute c_BF = x_A y_F - x_F y_A = x_A y_A (1 - U/(rS)) - (x_A + α/r) y_A = x_A y_A - x_A y_A U/(rS) - x_A y_A - (α/r) y_A = - y_A ( x_A U/(rS) + α/r ). So that is correct.

Thus the line BF equation is: (y_A U/(rS)) x + (α/r) y + ( - y_A ( x_A U/(rS) + α/r ) ) = 0? Wait, the line equation from determinant is: (y_A - y_F) x + (x_F - x_A) y + (x_A y_F - x_F y_A) = 0. So coefficient of x is y_A - y_F = y_A U/(rS). Coefficient of y is x_F - x_A = α/r. Constant term is x_A y_F - x_F y_A = - y_A ( x_A U/(rS) + α/r ). So line BF: (y_A U/(rS)) x + (α/r) y - y_A ( x_A U/(rS) + α/r ) = 0.

Now multiply by rS: (y_A U/(rS))*rS = y_A U, (α/r)*rS = α S, constant: - y_A ( x_A U/(rS) + α/r ) * rS = - y_A ( x_A U + α S ). So line BF: y_A U x + α S y - y_A ( x_A U + α S ) = 0. That is exactly the same as line BE we got. So it appears that both lines BE and BF have the same equation. But that would imply that points B, E, F are collinear, which they are not. So there must be a mistake in the line BE equation? Let's recompute line BE similarly.

Points: B(x_A, -y_A). E(x_A+α/R, y_A - (y_A U)/(RS)). So x_E = x_A + α/R, y_E = y_A (1 - U/(RS)). Then y_A - y_E = y_A - y_A (1 - U/(RS)) = y_A * U/(RS). So coefficient of x is y_A U/(RS). Coefficient of y is x_E - x_A = α/R. Constant term: x_A y_E - x_E y_A = x_A y_A (1 - U/(RS)) - (x_A + α/R) y_A = x_A y_A - x_A y_A U/(RS) - x_A y_A - (α/R) y_A = - y_A ( x_A U/(RS) + α/R ). So line BE: (y_A U/(RS)) x + (α/R) y - y_A ( x_A U/(RS) + α/R ) = 0. Multiply by RS: y_A U x + α S y - y_A ( x_A U + α S ) = 0. So indeed line BE is y_A U x + α S y - y_A ( x_A U + α S ) = 0. And line BF after multiplication gives the same equation. So it seems that both lines BE and BF are the same line. But in numeric, we computed slopes: BE slope = 1, BF slope = -1. So they are different. Let's compute the line BF equation using the determinant with numeric values to see if it matches the line BE equation.

Take numeric: d=5, r=3, R=4, x_A=1.8, y_A=2.4, α=2.4, S=12, U=7. Then line BE: y_A U x + α S y - y_A ( x_A U + α S ) = 2.4*7 x + 2.4*12 y - 2.4*(1.8*7 + 2.4*12) = 16.8 x + 28.8 y - 2.4*(12.6 + 28.8) = 16.8 x + 28.8 y - 2.4*41.4 = 16.8 x + 28.8 y - 99.36. Divide by 16.8: x + (28.8/16.8) y - 99.36/16.8 = x + 1.7142857 y - 5.9142857. That line has slope? Solve for y: 1.7142857 y = -x + 5.9142857 => y = -0.58333 x + 3.4545. That slope is -0.58333, not 1. So something is off. In numeric, we computed BE line: points B(1.8,-2.4), E(2.4,-1.8). Slope = (-1.8+2.4)/(2.4-1.8)=0.6/0.6=1. So line BE: y+2.4 = 1*(x-1.8) => y = x - 4.2. That line is y = x - 4.2. Does it satisfy the equation 16.8 x + 28.8 y - 99.36 = 0? Plug y = x-4.2: 16.8 x + 28.8 (x-4.2) - 99.36 = 16.8 x + 28.8 x - 120.96 - 99.36 = 45.6 x - 220.32 = 0 => x = 4.84, not identically zero. So the equation we derived for line BE is not correct because we used the determinant method but with the points B and E. Let's compute the line BE using determinant correctly.

The line through (x1,y1) and (x2,y2) is given by (y2-y1)x + (x1-x2)y + (x2 y1 - x1 y2)=0. That is the standard form. So for B(x_A, -y_A) and E(x_E, y_E), we have:

y_E - (-y_A) = y_E + y_A
x_A - x_E = x_A - x_E
constant: x_E * (-y_A) - x_A * y_E = -x_E y_A - x_A y_E

So the line equation is: (y_E + y_A) x + (x_A - x_E) y + (-x_E y_A - x_A y_E) = 0. That is the correct form. In our earlier determinant, we used (y_A - y_E) x + (x_E - x_A) y + (x_A y_E - x_E y_A)=0. That is also a valid form, but note that the coefficients are different; they are essentially the negative of the ones we have? Let's check: (y_A - y_E) = -(y_E - y_A). And (x_E - x_A) = -(x_A - x_E). And (x_A y_E - x_E y_A) = -(x_E y_A - x_A y_E). So the line equation from determinant is: -(y_E - y_A)x - (x_A - x_E)y - (x_E y_A - x_A y_E)=0, which is equivalent to (y_E - y_A)x + (x_A - x_E)y + (x_E y_A - x_A y_E)=0. So the standard form is: (y_E - y_A)x + (x_A - x_E)y + (x_E y_A - x_A y_E)=0. So we need to use that.

Thus for BE: y_E - y_A = (y_A - (y_A U)/(RS)) - y_A = - (y_A U)/(RS). So coefficient of x is - (y_A U)/(RS). Coefficient of y: x_A - x_E = x_A - (x_A + α/R) = - α/R. Constant: x_E y_A - x_A y_E = (x_A + α/R) y_A - x_A (y_A - (y_A U)/(RS)) = x_A y_A + (α/R) y_A - x_A y_A + x_A (y_A U)/(RS) = (α/R) y_A + x_A y_A U/(RS) = y_A (α/R + x_A U/(RS)). So line BE: - (y_A U)/(RS) x - (α/R) y + y_A (α/R + x_A U/(RS)) = 0. Multiply by -1: (y_A U)/(RS) x + (α/R) y - y_A (α/R + x_A U/(RS)) = 0. Multiply by RS: y_A U x + α S y - y_A (α S + x_A U) = 0. That is y_A U x + α S y - y_A (α S + x_A U) = 0. That's what we had. But for numeric, this gives: 2.4*7 x + 2.4*12 y - 2.4*(2.4*12 + 1.8*7) = 16.8 x + 28.8 y - 2.4*(28.8 + 12.6) = 16.8 x + 28.8 y - 2.4*41.4 = 16.8 x + 28.8 y - 99.36. So our derived line equation is not correct. The error is in the constant term: x_E y_A - x_A y_E. Let's compute that correctly: x_E = x_A + α/R, y_E = y_A - (y_A U)/(RS). Sum = 30.24 -69.12 -99.36 = -138.24. So it's not zero. But we expected it to be zero because we derived that equation from the line equation. But for B, we computed that and got -138.24, not zero. So B should satisfy - y_A U x_B - α S y_B + y_A (α S + x_A U)=0. Let's recompute the line equation using the determinant formula correctly.

The line through (x1,y1) and (x2,y2) is given by: (y2-y1)(x - x1) - (x2-x1)(y - y1)=0. So we can compute the equation in the form: (y2-y1)x - (x2-x1)y + (x2 y1 - x1 y2)=0. That is the standard form. So for B(x1,y1) and E(x2,y2), we have:

y2-y1 = y_E - (-y_A) = y_E + y_A = y_A - (y_A U)/(RS) + y_A = 2y_A - (y_A U)/(RS) = y_A (2 - U/(RS)).
x2-x1 = x_E - x_A = α/R.
x2 y1 - x1 y2 = x_E * (-y_A) - x_A * y_E = -x_E y_A - x_A y_E.

So the line equation is: (y_E + y_A) x - (α/R) y + (-x_E y_A - x_A y_E) = 0. That is: (y_A (2 - U/(RS))) x - (α/R) y - y_A (x_E + x_A U/(RS)? Actually compute -x_E y_A - x_A y_E = -y_A (x_E + x_A (1 - U/(RS)))? Wait, y_E = y_A (1 - U/(RS)). So -x_E y_A - x_A y_E = -y_A (x_E + x_A (1 - U/(RS))) = -y_A (x_A + α/R + x_A - x_A U/(RS)) = -y_A (2x_A + α/R - x_A U/(RS)). That is messy.

But we can also use the determinant method: The line equation is given by the determinant:

| x   y   1 |
| x_A -y_A 1 | = 0
| x_E y_E 1 |

Expanding: x*( -y_A - y_E ) - y*( x_A - x_E ) + 1*( x_A y_E - x_E (-y_A) ) = 0 => x*(-y_A - y_E) - y*(x_A - x_E) + (x_A y_E + x_E y_A) = 0 => - (y_A + y_E) x - (x_A - x_E) y + (x_A y_E + x_E y_A) = 0 => (y_A + y_E) x + (x_A - x_E) y - (x_A y_E + x_E y_A) = 0. So that is the correct form: (y_A + y_E) x + (x_A - x_E) y - (x_A y_E + x_E y_A) = 0. Now compute:

y_A + y_E = y_A + y_A (1 - U/(RS)) = 2y_A - (y_A U)/(RS) = y_A (2 - U/(RS)).
x_A - x_E = x_A - (x_A + α/R) = - α/R.
Constant: - (x_A y_E + x_E y_A) = - (x_A y_A (1 - U/(RS)) + (x_A + α/R) y_A) = - (x_A y_A - x_A y_A U/(RS) + x_A y_A + (α/R) y_A) = - (2x_A y_A + (α/R) y_A - x_A y_A U/(RS)) = - y_A (2x_A + α/R - x_A U/(RS)).

Thus line BE: y_A (2 - U/(RS)) x - (α/R) y - y_A (2x_A + α/R - x_A U/(RS)) = 0. Multiply by RS: y_A (2RS - U) x - α S y - y_A (2x_A RS + α S - x_A U) = 0? Not nice.

Now for numeric: y_A=2.4, U=7, RS=12, so 2 - U/(RS)=2 - 7/12=2 - 0.58333=1.41667. So coefficient of x = 2.4*1.41667=3.4. Then - (α/R) y = - (2.4/4) y = -0.6 y. Constant: - y_A (2x_A + α/R - x_A U/(RS)) = -2.4*(2*1.8 + 0.6 - 1.8*7/12) = -2.4*(3.6+0.6 - 1.05)= -2.4*(4.2-1.05)= -2.4*3.15= -7.56. So line BE: 3.4 x - 0.6 y - 7.56 = 0. Check B(1.8,-2.4): 3.4*1.8 -0.6*(-2.4)-7.56=6.12+1.44-7.56=0. So B satisfies. Good. So the correct line BE equation is: y_A (2 - U/(RS)) x - (α/R) y - y_A (2x_A + α/R - x_A U/(RS)) = 0. That is the form.

Now for BF, similarly: points B(x_A,-y_A) and F(x_A+α/r, y_A - (y_A U)/(rS)). Then y_A + y_F = y_A + y_A (1 - U/(rS)) = 2y_A - (y_A U)/(rS) = y_A (2 - U/(rS)). x_A - x_F = x_A - (x_A+α/r) = -α/r. Constant: - (x_A y_F + x_F y_A) = - (x_A y_A (1 - U/(rS)) + (x_A+α/r) y_A) = - y_A (x_A - x_A U/(rS) + x_A + α/r) = - y_A (2x_A + α/r - x_A U/(rS)). So line BF: y_A (2 - U/(rS)) x - (α/r) y - y_A (2x_A + α/r - x_A U/(rS)) = 0.

Now we have the two radical axis equations:

Ax+By+C+r^2 = λ (a_BE x + b_BE y + c_BE) for some λ, where a_BE = y_A (2 - U/(RS)), b_BE = -α/R, c_BE = - y_A (2x_A + α/R - x_A U/(RS)). But careful: The line equation we have is: a_BE x + b_BE y + c_BE = 0, with a_BE = y_A (2 - U/(RS)), b_BE = -α/R, c_BE = - y_A (2x_A + α/R - x_A U/(RS)). So we have:

A = λ a_BE
B = λ b_BE
C+r^2 = λ c_BE

Similarly, for BF: a_BF = y_A (2 - U/(rS)), b_BF = -α/r, c_BF = - y_A (2x_A + α/r - x_A U/(rS)). Then:

A+2d = μ a_BF
B = μ b_BF
C - d^2 + R^2 = μ c_BF

Now we can solve for A,B,C. This is still messy but maybe we can find that λ and μ are such that A and B are simple. Let's compute these using our expressions.

We have α = (U^2-d^2)V/(2d). And we have x_A = (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d). Also y_A^2 = (U^2-d^2)(d^2-V^2)/(4d^2). We can also express x_A and y_A in terms of d,r,R.

Let's try to understand the configuration better.

We have two circles Ω and Γ intersecting at A and B. Let O1 = M, O2 = N. The line O1O2 meets Ω at C and Γ at D, with order C-M-N-D. So M is between C and N, N between M and D. So C and D are external points.

Now P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. So P is the center of the circle through A, C, D. Since C and D are on line O1O2, the circle (ACD) has its center on the perpendicular bisector of CD. Let’s denote the midpoint of CD as K. Then PK ⟂ CD. So PK is perpendicular to O1O2.

Now consider triangle PMN. Since M and N are on CD, the side MN is part of CD. So the altitude from P in triangle PMN is PK (since PK ⟂ CD). So H lies on PK. Also, H is the orthocenter, so MH ⟂ PN and NH ⟂ PM.

Now line AP is a line through A and P. So AP is a line through A and the center of circle (ACD). This line meets Ω again at E and Γ again at F. So E and F are points on the circles such that A, P, E, F are collinear.

Now we need to show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF.

Observation: Since H is orthocenter of PMN, and AP is a line through A and P, maybe there is a relation that the reflection of H across the midpoint of MN lies on the circle with diameter something. Alternatively, note that the circle (BEF) might be the circle with diameter the segment joining the projections of H onto something.

Let's try to compute the circle (BEF) using complex numbers with A as origin? That might simplify because A is intersection of circles. Let's set A = 0 in complex plane. Now P is circumcenter of triangle ACD, with A=0. So P is the center of the circle through 0, C, D. So P is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors of 0C and 0D. Not exactly; the perpendicular bisector of 0C is the line through C/2 that is perpendicular to the vector C. So we can write P = C/2 + i * k * C, where k is real (since multiplying by i rotates by 90°). So P = C/2 + i * k * C = C(1/2 + i k). Similarly, from |P| = |P-D|, we get P = D/2 + i * l * D for some real l. So we have:

C(1/2 + i k) = D(1/2 + i l). Thus P is determined.

Now we also have that the line AP is the line through 0 and P, so it's the ray in direction P. So AP is the line through origin and P. So it's the set of points t P. Given the complexity, but we have already derived algebraic expressions in coordinates that worked. Perhaps we can present a coordinate proof that is clean and uses the derived simplifications. Let's try to compute the circle (BEF) in terms of the parameters using the coordinates we already have, but with the correct line equations. Then compute the distance condition.

We have coordinates:

M = (0,0)
N = (d,0)
A = (x_A, y_A) with x_A = (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d), y_A = sqrt(r^2 - x_A^2)
B = (x_A, -y_A)

C = (-r, 0)
D = (d+R, 0)

P = (x_P, y_P) with x_P = (d+R-r)/2, y_P = - (S (d^2 - V^2))/(4d y_A) where S = d+R+r, V = R-r.

v = P - A = (Δx, Δy) = (x_P - x_A, y_P - y_A). We computed Δx = V S/(2d) = (R-r)(d+R+r)/(2d). Δy = - (d^2 - V^2)U/(4d^2 y_A) = - (d^2 - (R-r)^2)(R+r)/(4d^2 y_A). Also, we have y_A^2 = (U^2-d^2)(d^2-V^2)/(4d^2). So we can simplify Δy.

Now H = (x_P, y_H) with y_H = - (d y_A)/S.

Now line L: through H with direction v. So L: (x,y) = H + λ v.

Now we need to find the circumcircle of B, E, F. We have E = A + t_E v, with t_E = (U^2-d^2)/(R S). F = A + t_F v, with t_F = (U^2-d^2)/(r S). So E and F are points on line AP. So we have coordinates for B, E, F.

Now we can compute the circle (BEF) by finding its center O = (X0, Y0). Since B and E are on Ω, and B and F are on Γ, maybe we can find that the circle (BEF) is the circle with diameter the segment connecting the points (x_A, -y_A) and something? Not.

Let's compute the circumcenter O using perpendicular bisectors of BE and BF. We already have expressions for B and E. Let's compute the midpoint of BE and the slope of BE.

B = (x_A, -y_A)
E = (x_A + t_E Δx, y_A + t_E Δy)

We have t_E Δx = (U^2-d^2)/(R S) * (V S/(2d)) = (U^2-d^2)V/(2d R). Similarly, t_E Δy = - (y_A U)/(R S) from earlier? Actually we computed t_E Δy = - (y_A U)/(R S). So E = (x_A + (U^2-d^2)V/(2d R), y_A - (y_A U)/(R S)). Similarly, F = (x_A + (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r), y_A - (y_A U)/(r S)).

Now we can compute the perpendicular bisector of BE. The midpoint M_BE = ((x_A + E_x)/2, (-y_A + E_y)/2) = (x_A + (U^2-d^2)V/(4d R), -y_A/2 + y_A/2 - (y_A U)/(2R S)) = (x_A + (U^2-d^2)V/(4d R), - (y_A U)/(2R S)). So M_BE = (x_A + (U^2-d^2)V/(4d R), - (y_A U)/(2R S)). The slope of BE: (E_y - (-y_A))/(E_x - x_A) = (y_A - (y_A U)/(R S) + y_A)/( (U^2-d^2)V/(2d R) ) = (2y_A - (y_A U)/(R S)) * (2d R)/( (U^2-d^2)V ) = y_A (2 - U/(R S)) * (2d R)/( (U^2-d^2)V ). But note that U = R+r, and we have relation: (U^2-d^2) = (R+r)^2 - d^2. Also, V = R-r. So the slope is messy.

Similarly, for BF: midpoint M_BF = (x_A + (U^2-d^2)V/(4d r), - (y_A U)/(2r S)). Slope of BF: (F_y - (-y_A))/(F_x - x_A) = (2y_A - (y_A U)/(r S)) / ( (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r) ) = y_A (2 - U/(r S)) * (2d r)/( (U^2-d^2)V ).

Now the perpendicular bisector of BE has slope = -1/(slope of BE). So it's very messy.

Given the algebraic complexity, but we can try to compute the circle (BEF) using the radical axis method we set up earlier. That gave us linear equations for A,B,C. Perhaps we can solve for A and B in terms of the knowns and then compute the circle's center and radius. That might be doable with some algebra.

Let's attempt to compute the circle (BEF) directly using the general formula for circumcircle of three points. We have coordinates for B, E, F. We can compute the circumcenter by solving the linear system for the circle equation: x^2+y^2+Ax+By+C=0. We have three equations:

(1) x_A^2+y_A^2 + A x_A + B(-y_A) + C = 0 => r^2 + A x_A - B y_A + C = 0.
(2) E_x^2+E_y^2 + A E_x + B E_y + C = 0.
(3) F_x^2+F_y^2 + A F_x + B F_y + C = 0.

We can subtract (1) from (2) and (3) to eliminate C. That gives:

(2') E_x^2+E_y^2 - r^2 + A(E_x - x_A) + B(E_y + y_A) = 0.
(3') F_x^2+F_y^2 - r^2 + A(F_x - x_A) + B(F_y + y_A) = 0.

Now we need to compute E_x^2+E_y^2 and F_x^2+F_y^2. Since E lies on Ω, we have |E|^2 = r^2? Actually, Ω is centered at M=(0,0) with radius r, so for any point on Ω, x^2+y^2 = r^2. So E is on Ω, so E_x^2+E_y^2 = r^2. Similarly, F is on Γ, so (F_x-d)^2+F_y^2 = R^2, but not necessarily x^2+y^2 = r^2. So E_x^2+E_y^2 = r^2. So (2') becomes: r^2 - r^2 + A(E_x - x_A) + B(E_y + y_A) = 0 => A(E_x - x_A) + B(E_y + y_A) = 0. Similarly, (3') becomes: F_x^2+F_y^2 - r^2 + A(F_x - x_A) + B(F_y + y_A) = 0. But we can also use that F lies on Γ: (F_x-d)^2+F_y^2 = R^2. That might help.

So we have:

A(E_x - x_A) + B(E_y + y_A) = 0.   (2'')

Now E_x - x_A = t_E Δx = (U^2-d^2)V/(2d R). And E_y + y_A = (y_A - (y_A U)/(R S)) + y_A = 2y_A - (y_A U)/(R S) = y_A (2 - U/(R S)). So (2'') gives:

A * (U^2-d^2)V/(2d R) + B * y_A (2 - U/(R S)) = 0.   (2''')

Similarly, for F: we have F_x - x_A = t_F Δx = (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r). And F_y + y_A = 2y_A - (y_A U)/(r S) = y_A (2 - U/(r S)). So (3') gives:

F_x^2+F_y^2 - r^2 + A(F_x - x_A) + B(F_y + y_A) = 0. But we can also compute F_x^2+F_y^2 using that F lies on Γ: (F_x-d)^2+F_y^2 = R^2. So F_x^2+F_y^2 = R^2 + 2d F_x - d^2. So F_x^2+F_y^2 - r^2 = R^2 + 2d F_x - d^2 - r^2. But we also have relation from B: x_A^2+y_A^2 = r^2, and (x_A-d)^2+y_A^2 = R^2. So R^2 - r^2 = (x_A-d)^2 - x_A^2 = -2d x_A + d^2. So R^2 = r^2 -2d x_A + d^2. Then R^2 + 2d F_x - d^2 - r^2 = (r^2 -2d x_A + d^2) + 2d F_x - d^2 - r^2 = 2d(F_x - x_A). So F_x^2+F_y^2 - r^2 = 2d(F_x - x_A). That is nice! Because F_x - x_A = (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r). So then F_x^2+F_y^2 - r^2 = 2d * (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r) = (U^2-d^2)V/r.

Thus (3') becomes: (U^2-d^2)V/r + A(F_x - x_A) + B(F_y + y_A) = 0. Substitute F_x - x_A = (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r). So:

(U^2-d^2)V/r + A * (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r) + B * y_A (2 - U/(r S)) = 0.

Multiply both sides by r: (U^2-d^2)V + A * (U^2-d^2)V/(2d) + B * y_A r (2 - U/(r S)) = 0.

But note that (U^2-d^2)V = (U^2-d^2)(R-r). So we have:

A * (U^2-d^2)V/(2d) + B * y_A r (2 - U/(r S)) = - (U^2-d^2)V.

Now we also have (2'''): A * (U^2-d^2)V/(2d R) + B * y_A (2 - U/(R S)) = 0.

So we have two linear equations in A and B. Let's denote:

Let K = (U^2-d^2)V/(2d). Then (2'''): A * K/R + B * y_A (2 - U/(R S)) = 0. (Eq1)
And (3'): A * K/r + B * y_A r (2 - U/(r S)) = - (U^2-d^2)V = -2d K? Because K = (U^2-d^2)V/(2d), so (U^2-d^2)V = 2d K. So RHS = -2d K.

Thus (Eq2): A * K/r + B * y_A r (2 - U/(r S)) = -2d K.

Now we can solve for A and B. Multiply (Eq1) by something to eliminate A or B.

From (Eq1): A * K/R = - B * y_A (2 - U/(R S)). So A = - (R/K) B y_A (2 - U/(R S)). Substitute into (Eq2):

- (R/K) B y_A (2 - U/(R S)) * (K/r) + B y_A r (2 - U/(r S)) = -2d K.

Simplify: - (R/r) B y_A (2 - U/(R S)) + B y_A r (2 - U/(r S)) = -2d K.

Factor B y_A: B y_A [ - (R/r)(2 - U/(R S)) + r (2 - U/(r S)) ] = -2d K.

Now compute the bracket:

- (R/r)(2 - U/(R S)) + r (2 - U/(r S)) = - (2R/r) + (R/r)*(U/(R S)) + 2r - r*(U/(r S)) = - (2R/r) + (U/(r S)) + 2r - (U/S).

Combine terms: = 2r - (2R/r) + (U/(r S)) - (U/S) = 2r - (2R/r) + (U/S)(1/r - 1) = 2r - (2R/r) + (U/S)((1 - r)/r). So bracket = 2r - (2R/r) + (U(1-r))/(r S).

Now note that U = R+r, S = d+R+r. So we have an expression. This still looks messy. But perhaps we can simplify further using the relation between d, r, R. Since circles intersect, we have d^2 = (R+r)^2 - 4Rr cos^2? Not.

Maybe we can find that A and B are such that the circle (BEF) has a simple equation. Let's try to compute the circumcircle of BEF using the fact that B is on both circles, and E and F are on lines through A. Perhaps there is a known result: The circle through B, E, F is the circle with diameter the segment joining the projections of H onto something. Alternatively, we can try to compute the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) and show that it equals the square of the distance from H to the line through H parallel to AP? That doesn't make sense.

Given the difficulty, but we have already derived that in the numeric, the line L is tangent. So we can attempt to prove that the distance from the center of circle (BEF) to L equals its radius. That is a computational approach. We can compute the center O of circle (BEF) using the perpendicular bisector method but with the simplified expressions we have for B, E, F. Perhaps we can find that O has a simple expression in terms of d, r, R.

Let's try to compute O by solving the linear system for the circle equation. We have:

(1) r^2 + A x_A - B y_A + C = 0.
(2) A(E_x - x_A) + B(E_y + y_A) = 0.
(3) A(F_x - x_A) + B(F_y + y_A) = - (F_x^2+F_y^2 - r^2) = -2d(F_x - x_A) from earlier? Actually we used that F_x^2+F_y^2 - r^2 = 2d(F_x - x_A). So (3) becomes: A(F_x - x_A) + B(F_y + y_A) = -2d(F_x - x_A). That is a linear equation in A and B.

Thus we have two linear equations:

(2) A * (U^2-d^2)V/(2d R) + B * y_A (2 - U/(R S)) = 0.
(3) A * (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r) + B * y_A (2 - U/(r S)) = -2d * (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r) = - (U^2-d^2)V/r.

Now we can solve for A and B. Let's denote:

Let α1 = (U^2-d^2)V/(2d R). Let β1 = y_A (2 - U/(R S)). Then (2): α1 A + β1 B = 0.
Let α2 = (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r). Let β2 = y_A (2 - U/(r S)). Then (3): α2 A + β2 B = - (U^2-d^2)V/r = -2d α2? Because α2 = (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r), so (U^2-d^2)V/r = 2d α2. So RHS = -2d α2.

Thus we have:

α1 A + β1 B = 0   (I)
α2 A + β2 B = -2d α2   (II)

We can solve for A and B. From (I): A = - (β1/α1) B. Substitute into (II): α2 (-β1/α1) B + β2 B = -2d α2 => B (β2 - (α2 β1)/α1) = -2d α2 => B = -2d α2 / (β2 - (α2 β1)/α1). Then A = - (β1/α1) B.

Now we need to compute α1, β1, α2, β2. Let's compute these in terms of d,r,R.

First, U = R+r, V = R-r, S = d+R+r.

Then:

α1 = (U^2-d^2)V/(2d R) = ((R+r)^2-d^2)(R-r)/(2d R).
α2 = (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r) = ((R+r)^2-d^2)(R-r)/(2d r).

β1 = y_A (2 - U/(R S)) = y_A (2 - (R+r)/(R S)).
β2 = y_A (2 - U/(r S)) = y_A (2 - (R+r)/(r S)).

Now we also have y_A^2 = (U^2-d^2)(d^2-V^2)/(4d^2). So y_A = sqrt((U^2-d^2)(d^2-V^2))/(2d). But we might not need explicit y_A if we can simplify the expressions.

Now compute β2 - (α2 β1)/α1.

First, α2/α1 = ( (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r) ) / ( (U^2-d^2)V/(2d R) ) = R/r. So α2/α1 = R/r.

Thus β2 - (α2 β1)/α1 = β2 - (R/r) β1.

So B = -2d α2 / (β2 - (R/r) β1). Then A = - (β1/α1) B = - (β1/α1) * (-2d α2 / (β2 - (R/r) β1)) = 2d α2 β1/(α1 (β2 - (R/r) β1)) = 2d (α2/α1) β1/(β2 - (R/r) β1) = 2d (R/r) β1/(β2 - (R/r) β1).

Now we can compute A and B. But we need to compute the circle's center O = (-A/2, -B/2). So we need to compute these.

Let's compute β1 and β2 explicitly.

β1 = y_A (2 - (R+r)/(R S)) = y_A (2 - U/(R S)).
β2 = y_A (2 - U/(r S)).

Now compute β2 - (R/r) β1 = y_A [2 - U/(r S) - (R/r)(2 - U/(R S))] = y_A [2 - U/(r S) - (2R/r) + (R/r)*(U/(R S))] = y_A [2 - (2R/r) - U/(r S) + U/(r S)] = y_A [2 - (2R/r)] = 2y_A (1 - R/r). So β2 - (R/r) β1 = 2y_A (1 - R/r) = -2y_A (R/r - 1). That is nice! So the denominator simplifies.

Thus B = -2d α2 / ( -2y_A (R/r - 1) ) = (2d α2) / (2y_A (R/r - 1)) = d α2 / (y_A (R/r - 1)).

Now α2 = (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r). So B = d * (U^2-d^2)V/(2d r) / (y_A (R/r - 1)) = (U^2-d^2)V/(2r) / (y_A (R/r - 1)) = (U^2-d^2)V/(2r y_A (R/r - 1)). Note that R/r - 1 = (R-r)/r = V/r. So B = (U^2-d^2)V/(2r y_A * (V/r)) = (U^2-d^2)V/(2r y_A) * (r/V) = (U^2-d^2)/(2 y_A). So B = (U^2-d^2)/(2 y_A). That's a nice simple expression!

Now compute A = 2d (R/r) β1/(β2 - (R/r) β1) = 2d (R/r) β1 / (2y_A (1 - R/r))? Wait, we had denominator = β2 - (R/r) β1 = 2y_A (1 - R/r). So A = 2d (R/r) β1 / (2y_A (1 - R/r)) = d (R/r) β1 / (y_A (1 - R/r)). But note that 1 - R/r = (r-R)/r = -V/r. So denominator = y_A * (-V/r) = - y_A V/r. So A = d (R/r) β1 / (- y_A V/r) = d (R/r) β1 * (-r/(y_A V)) = - d R β1/(y_A V). Now β1 = y_A (2 - U/(R S)). So A = - d R * y_A (2 - U/(R S)) / (y_A V) = - d R (2 - U/(R S))/V = - d R (2 - U/(R S))/V.

Now simplify: 2 - U/(R S) = (2R S - U)/(R S). So A = - d R * (2R S - U)/(R S) / V = - d (2R S - U)/(S V). So A = - d (2R S - U)/(S V).

Now we have A and B in terms of d, r, R, S, U, V. Recall that S = d+R+r, U = R+r, V = R-r.

Now we can also find C from (1): C = -r^2 - A x_A + B y_A. But we might not need C for the circle's center and radius. The center O = (-A/2, -B/2). So:

O_x = -A/2 = d (2R S - U)/(2 S V)
O_y = -B/2 = - (U^2-d^2)/(2 y_A)

Line L: through H = (x_P, y_H) with direction v = (Δx, Δy). So L: (x,y) = H + λ v.

We need to compute distance from O to L: dist = |(O-H) × v| / |v|. And we need to show that this equals r_c.

We have expressions for O, H, v. This is still heavy but maybe simplifies.

Now O_x - x_P = d (2R S - U)/(2 S V) - (d+V)/2 = (1/2)[ d (2R S - U)/(S V) - (d+V) ].

Now we have expressions for x_A, y_A, etc. This is still heavy, but maybe we can find that the cross product simplifies to something like (U^2-d^2) * something, and |v| simplifies, and r_c simplifies to something like (U^2-d^2)/(2 y_A)? Not.

Let's compute r_c^2 using the circle equation. Since we have A and B, we can compute r_c^2 = (A^2+B^2)/4 - C. But we also have that the circle passes through B, so r_c^2 = (x_A - O_x)^2 + (-y_A - O_y)^2. That might be easier to compute if we find O_x and O_y.

Maybe we can find that O_x = x_P + something? Let's compute O_x in terms of x_P and other parameters.

This is reminiscent of the expression we had for |v|^2 earlier. We previously computed |v|^2 = Rr S^2/(U^2-d^2)? Actually we computed |v|^2 = Rr S^2/(U^2-d^2) from earlier? Now we also have O_y = - (U^2-d^2)/(2 y_A). And we have O_y - y_H = O_y + (d y_A)/S = - (U^2-d^2)/(2 y_A) + (d y_A)/S.

Maybe we can find that O_x - x_P and O_y - y_H have simple forms.

Compute O_x - x_P = d (2R S - U)/(2 S V) - (d+V)/2 = (1/2)[ d (2R S - U)/(S V) - (d+V) ].

Let’s compute the term inside: d (2R S - U)/(S V) - (d+V). Write as:

d (2R S - U)/(S V) - d - V = d[ (2R S - U)/(S V) - 1 ] - V = d[ (2R S - U - S V)/(S V) ] - V = d[ (2R S - U - S V)/(S V) ] - V.

Now compute 2R S - U - S V. Since S = d+R+r, V = R-r, U = R+r. So:

2R S - U - S V = 2R(d+R+r) - (R+r) - (d+R+r)(R-r) = 2Rd + 2R^2 + 2Rr - R - r - (d+R+r)(R-r). Expand (d+R+r)(R-r) = d(R-r) + (R+r)(R-r) = d(R-r) + (R^2 - r^2). So:

2Rd + 2R^2 + 2Rr - R - r - d(R-r) - (R^2 - r^2) = 2Rd + 2R^2 + 2Rr - R - r - dR + d r - R^2 + r^2 = (2Rd - dR) + (2R^2 - R^2) + (2Rr) + (d r) - R - r + r^2 = dR + R^2 + 2Rr + d r - R - r + r^2.

Combine terms: = R^2 + 2Rr + r^2 + dR + d r - R - r = (R+r)^2 + d(R+r) - (R+r) = (R+r)(R+r + d - 1). So 2R S - U - S V = U (U + d - 1). Because U = R+r. So then:

O_x - x_P = (1/2)[ d * (U (U+d-1))/(S V) - V ] = (1/2)[ d U (U+d-1)/(S V) - V ].

That is not simpler.
